= =!
The National Textbook Project
— =
By issuing the textbooks for the third year of each educational cycle, the Educational Center
for Research and Development will have completed the third and last installment of books called
for by the New Curricula. We are placing these books in the hands of students with the great
hope that we are moving, step by step, toward the goal of acquiring sound learning, using
sophisticated educational means and up-to-date methodology that encourage and reinforce
individual thinking and research, acquisition of skills, development of ethical and national
attitudes, the feeling of national belonging as well as the feeling of belonging to humanity at
large.
The on-going revolution in information, communication and educational-aids technology has
undoubtedly limited the role of the textbook and lowered the rank it used so recently to occupy.
However, in our society and in many other societies, the textbook remains the basic means of
education, and it is our duty to exert our utmost effort and care to come up with the best product
as to form and content. Yet we should not lose sight of the fact that the textbook is not sufficient
by itself but should rather be used as a stepping stone to access other sources of information.
What is important is to keep a clear vision and maintain the right course toward our objective. |
The means should not turn into the end and the student should always remain the focus of the
learning/teaching process. |
No one ignores or denies the fact that textbook writing requires very high academic and
educational qualifications and very wide field experience. The authors committees undeniably
possess such qualities. Yet the textbooks of the last two years contained some negative
aspects. Such is the nature of human work, no matter how good the intentions or how great the
effort extended. Here constructive criticism constitutes a real contribution to raising the standard
of authorship, minimizing errors and filling gaps. We say that, with all due appreciation and
respect to all those who have contributed to the success of this project.
The Educational Center for Research and Development is embarking this year on a process
of evaluating the New Curricula and related textbooks, teacher training courses and student
achievement. This is a natural and necessary step now that the new system has been put into
effect. This process aims at identifying the curricular objectives that have been achieved as well
as those that have not been achieved, with a view to proceeding with the positive aspects and
correcting the negative ones.
As part of this correction process, we plan to review the versions that have been issued in
order to secure good textbooks for our students, who always deserve the best.
March 13, 2000 President, Educational Center for
Research and Development
Nemer FRAYHA
Lc
===END PAGE 7===

===PAGE 8===
= aa PREFACE

The third year of seondary education is the last year in this stage. The mathematics book for

“The General Sciences” consists of two parts: a part that deals with themes related to alge-

bra and geometry, and a part that deals with calculus, probability, and statistics. The authors

decided to dedicate a book to each part with the view that this division, although not quite
realistic in mathematics, will make the book easier and more practical for the student and
teacher to work with.

The two books continue to follow the same pattern as the other books . They were created

and edited in compliance with the demands of the new program. Here we find: the scientif-

ic content, unambiguously detailed and defined; the targeted objectives, controlled and eval-
uated; and the explicity shown method recommended.

Far from being a dry account of facts, this work is a tool that permits and promotes the active

participation of learners. We wanted to create an instructive product suitable for an education

based on the balanced and well-defined division of labor between three parties: the student -
the instructor - the administrator.

The instructor is not only the possessor of knowledge but is more the leader of the class;

he/she guides it in its choice of orientations, maintains its interest, promotes research among

its members, and helps find results while indicating their applications.

At the heart of the educative system is the student, a person with the right to an education

worthy of a free individual, independent and seeking knowledge. It is the student who, with-

out feeling forced to put up with the knowledge he/she must learn, should feel constantly
faced with situations that stimulate his/her desire to know more.

* In what concerns new notions (such as logic) that assume that the student has a high level
of maturity, the appeal will be to his/her intuition and not to his/her reasoning.

* Pleasant and easy proofs were kept in the text while those that seemed to us complucated
and difficult were omitted. Our objective was to introduce the student to reasoning and to
prepare hinVher to tackle the exercises at the end of the chapter with ease.

* The “Just for Fun” heading closes each chapter of the book. This will be the focus of a
weekly session reserved for independent mathematical research with a theme (that the
teacher will choose or modify according to the level of the class). Some may think that this
approach is “ not classical” and so oppose it by naming it a “waste of time.” We reply,
however, that the benefits of such research by far compensate for the relative length of
time allocated to it.

* This year, a new heading appears in some chapters. It is the heading “Selected Reading.”
It presents the student with historical notes and useful comments.

Finally, any remarks, criticisms, or suggestions that colleagues would like to make will be

received with attention.

The authors

— Q LoGic
INTRODUCTION -———
Man’s interest in logic goes back to ancient times. The Greeks
were the first to give this branch of knowledge its place of
honor, Logic is found at the very base of reasoning.
Unfortunately however, the intuitive manipulation of this dis-
cipline, that deals with statements, may lead to contradictory
results, Hence the necessity to introduce specific rules. For a
long time handling these rules remained difficult and arduous,
but the development of algebra allowed a relatively simple
presentation of logic. The basic idea is to consider a statement
as a variable that may have one value of two : T (if true) and

F (if false).

Prerequ isites Formal logic is only used in mathematics; itis widely used in
concepts of electronic circuits, called logic circuits, which are
used to manufacture many electronic machines (particularly

Although this chapter computers).

does not require any = ¥

“a ey This chapter intends to : :

specific knowledge,

knowledge of the 1. specify the notion of statements;

syllabus onyp receding 2. state the rules that guide the use of logical connectives;

classes is necessary In L. . .

solving the examples 3. explicitly explain the fundamental rules of logic.

and exercises.

@ STATEMENTS
In logic, a statement is a sentence characterized by the fact that
it is either true or false.
—®: Examples —@£  @@.  $_ i
1. “Beirut is an Asian city" is a statement since it is a true sentence.
2. "Every isosceles triangle is right" is a false statement.
3. "What time is it ?" is not a statement,
(TRUTH VALUES
To each statement p, assign the letter 7 if it is true and the letter
F if it is false. The letter 7 or F is called the truth value of the — F is the truth value of the
statement p. statement «x is a rationnel
; . . . . number.
The truth value of a statement complies with the following two
principles :
The principle of contradiction : A statement cannot be
true and false at the same time.
The principle of excluded middle : A statement is either
true or false and nothing else.
The preceding text enables us to think of a statement as a
variable that takes two values in the set {T,F}.A statement _
is defined when its truth value is stated. Thus, it is possible" ‘#!culate the truth value of a
:, . composite statement, we use a
to create new statements from others by defining their truth table called the truth table.
values in terms of those statements that form them.

The Gaussian elimination method is based on the following
fundamental result ;
The elementary operations are a roperty 2

Let, _ Every elementary operation transforms a linear system into

L.— al, with « #0 an equivalent linear system.

L,—al,+BL, with a 40.
@ GAUSSIAN ELIMINATION
This consists of transforming a linear system (S) into an echelon
system (S’) through the application of successive elementary
operations. By the preceding property, the system (S’) is
equivalent to the system (S). It is therefore enough to solve (S") to
obtain the solutions of (S).
To make things easier, denote by L,, L., ..., L,, the equations of the
system and by x,, x,, ....., the unknowns. Gaussian elimination
consists of the following steps :

1. If the coefficient of x, in L, is zero, interchange L, with

This nonzero coefficient is called i e A é

the first pivot of Gauss. another equation such that the coefficient of x, in L, is
different from zero.

The first col thus reduced eB ‘ ‘

tb Seen ore 2. Set the coefficients of x, equal to zero in all the equations
L,, L,, L,,. Let (S') be the system obtained.

3. Repeat Steps | and 2 for the linear system (m — 1) x (n — 1)
that consists of the equations L',, L', ..., L',.

Denote by (S") the echelon system obtained after reduction to
echelon form. Three cases may be considered : «

1. The echelon system (S") is perfectly triangular. In this case
the system has a unique solution.

2. The echelon system (S") contains an equation whose first
side is zero and whose second side is nonzero. In this case
the system has no solution.

3. The echelon system (S") is trapezoidal with r equations. In
this case the system has an infinite number of solutions.

This method shall be illustrated using three examples .
—+t axe
Solve the system
x—2y=—1
(s) =x+3y=5
sx — 8y=0.
Start by reducing the system to echelon form :

The echelon system obtained is
xty-32=4
(s') 32 = 0
(=o
ee The system (S’) is a system of two equations in three unknowns. In this case,
Ss the unknowns are classified in two classes: the main (principal) unknowns
and the secondary unknowns, The main unknowns are the first unknowns
with nonzero coefficients in the different equations. Their number is equal to
the number of equations of the echelon system.The secondary unknowns are
the other unknowns.The main unknowns in our system are x and z. There is a
single secondary unknown: y.
To solve such a system, we transpose the secondary unknowns to the other side
of the equation and deal with them as though they were parameters. This
operation transforms the system (S") into the system
x~-3ze4a—-y
5z=0
===END PAGE 26===

===PAGE 27===
The main unknowns are x and y. There is only one secondary
unknown which is z. We transpose z to the other side. We obtain
For z = 0, we have the solution xtyaiz+l
x=0,y=Landz=0. sm] ah
For z = 1, we have the solution ®
x=5,y=—Jandz=1 The solution of this system gives x = 5z, y= 1 — 4z andz=z.
To every given value of z there corresponds a solution of the
system. Thus the system has an infinite number of solutions.
1. To reduce a linear system to echelon form, apply the following algorithm:
a) If the coefficient of x, in L, is zero, interchange L, with another equation such that the
coefficient of x,in L, is different from zero.
b) Set the coefficients of x, equal to zero in all the equations L,, L,,..., L,. Let (S’) be the
system obtained.
c) Repeat Steps a) and b) for the linear system (m — 1) x (n —1) consisting of the
equations L’,, L’,,..., L’,.
2. To solve a linear system, proceed as follows:
a) Reduce the system to echelon form.
b) Solve the system obtained.
3. To solve a linear system that depends on a parameter m proceed as follows:
a) Reduce the system to echelon form.
b) Investigate the system for the values of m that make the last pivot of Gauss equal to
zero:

i) Specify the values of m for which the system has no solution.

ii) For the values of m for which the system has an infinite number of solutions, deter-
mine the main unknowns and the secondary unknowns, and calculate the main
unknowns in terms of the secondary unknowns.

c) For the other values of m, calculate the unique solution in terms of m.

— 4) PLANES AND LINES
INTRODUCTION —H—
In our first and second secondary years, analytic geometry
enabled us to express geometric properties in the plane as
equations or inequalities (lines,circles, discs ...).
This year we shall use analytic geometry and some of its tools
(the scalar product, vector product, and triple scalar product)
to equally express some geometric properties in space as equa-
tions or systems of equations .
From these equations that represent some determined geomet-
ric figures, we shall later deal with orthogonality and paral-
lelism in space .
Prerequisites In this chapter, we shall determine :
1. an equation of a plane given by geometric elements;
Knowledge of the: 2. a system of equations of a line also given by geometric
Scalar product. elements.
~ Vector product.
- Triple scalar product.

__ PARALLELISM AND

INTRODUCTION ————
The main target of this chapter is to make use of the acquired
knowledge of solid geometry, analytic and vector tools in the
Prerequis ites study of parallelism and orthogonality.
Their use will allow us to easily determine the relative posi-
. tion of a line and a plane and to specify their intersections. It
Knowledge’ ob thes will also facilitate the calculation of the distance from a point

angle between (d) and (P) is the complement of the acute
angle determined by the vectors m and m’.
d) The vectors n and m have the same direction. In this case,
vector m is normal to (P) and line (d) is perpendicular to this
plane. This occurs if and only if there exists a nonzero real
number f such that 7m = ¢m or u = ta, v = th, and w = tc.

INTRODUCTION —M——
The study of the sphere ( its position, intersection with lines,
surfaces, or solids) is somewhat demanding. For this reason
we shall resort to the use of analytic calculation and the equa-
tion of the sphere in space. This equation is a tool used to ana-
me lytically study geometric properties, such as the position of a
Prerequisites point with respect to a sphere, as well as the intersection of a
sphere with a line, a plane, or another sphere, by using the
Knowledge of the: equations of lines and planes.
Sphere its definition In this chapter, we shall:
and properties, 1. determine the equation of a sphere:
Sealer product in space: 2. discuss the position of a point with respect to a sphere;
Equation of a plane. 3. determine the relative position of a sphere with respect to
Equations of a line. a line, plane, or another sphere and find the elements of
their intersection when they exist.

INTRODUCTION —H—
A triangle is formed of six elements: three angles and three
sides. It is well-known that a unique triangle can always be
Prerequisites constructed if three of its elements are given, at least one of
them is a side. This shows that three exists three distinct rela-
Knowledge of: tions between the six elements of triangle.
Metric relations in a The knowldge of such relations plays a basic role in Geodesy,
right triangle in the domain of cadastre, etc. We have already seen them in
The area of a triangle a right triangle. We shall generalize them, to any triangle.
The dot product.
In this chapter, we shall estabish:
The vector product. . .
1. two fundamental systems of relations in a triangle;
The trigonometric func- . . . . .
Hei: 2. other relations that give the radius of the circumscribed
circle and the area of the triangle.

INTRODUCTION —M—
Various problems lead to the solution of trigonometric equa-
tions (for instance, the study of trigonometric functions). In
physics these equations are fundamental for finding the posi-
tions of relative equilibrium of a physical system in motion; in
mathematics, they are necessary for the study of the zeros and
the sign of the derivative of circular functions.
To solve such equations, we have at our disposal the various
rules of trigonometric calculations and examples of trigono-
metric equations that were studied in the second year secon-
Prerequisites dary.
Knowledge of the: In this chapter, we will:
© Elementary irigono- 1. solve equations containing only one trigonometric func-
smnbae equations tion of the unknown arc;
, . - 2. solve the classical equation acos x + bsin x = c;
Trigonometric formulas
3. solve trigonometric equations that lead to these last two.

that I know!

that I know

® TRIGONOMETRIC APPLICATIONS
1. Linearization of trigonometric polynomials
A trigonometric polynomial is the sum of terms of the type
acos" x sin’ x where a is a real number, n and p are two natural
numbers.
To linearize a trigonometric polynomial is to express it as a sum
of terms of the type bcos(gx) or csin (q’x) (b and ¢ are two real
numbers g and q’ are two natural numbers).
Using Euler’s formulas
cos = eves and sin@ = ae

EXPONENTIAL FORM
OF A COMPLEX NUMBER
INTRODUCTION ———
The algebraic and trigonometric forms of a complex number
have already been introduced.
In the eighteenth century, the British mathematician De
Prerequisites Moivre discovered the formula that now bears his name and
that turned out to be an essential tool in trigonometric calcula-
tions. The famous Swiss mathematician Euler also proved
Knowledge of: : = : e
formulas that linked exponential and trigonometric functions.

trigonometric forms In this chapter, we will:
of a complex number. 1. introduce the exponential form of a nonzero trigonometric

The binomial formula. 2. prove De Moivre’s formula;
3. provide an effective trigonometric tool.

SELECTED READING
THE ORIGIN OF COMPLEX NUMBERS

Complex numbers have their origin in the solution of equations, In the XVIth century, mathematicians knew
that some quadratic equations did not have roots (in the set of real numbers). This knowlwdge did not pose any
problem however. The discovery of formulas that gave the roots of the cubic equation by the Italian mathemati-
cian Cardan (or Cardano) triggered a real crisis. Cardan proved that the roots of the equation x° + px + q = 0
could be calculated by using the formula

2 3 2 3
ger Oy Ee Ae lg oe
2 4 27 2 4 27

If the roots of the equation x’ — 15x — 4 = 0,are to be calculated, a mathematician finds that Cardan’s for-
mula leads to “impossible” numbers. Indeed, we obtain

anne ——
x =V2+ V=121 + ¥2= V=121.

Up until then one could still say, as could be said for quadratic equations, that the equation has no roots. But
a simple verification shows that 4 is a root of this equation. To break this deadlock, the mathematician Bombelli
showed that by using the symbol \—1, it was possible to prove the equality

es ———_
4=V2+ V-121 + ¥2—- V—121.
He proved the following two inequalities
ee eet pu aa SeeN
2-V=121 = (2 +\=1) and 2—V—-121 = (2 a \=1) :
that yielded
$$ ——=_ aos i
2+ V= tar + ¥2— Vial =24+V—14+2-\-1 =4,

Since then, mathematicians learned how to manipulate the symbols \=a where a> 0. Such symbols were a
means that enables them to make calculations that were previously impossible. These symbols were called “imag-
inary numbers” without being accepted as numbers by the mathematics community.

In the XIXth century, with the arrival of Gauss and Cauchy, complex numbers were given their representation
among “the known mathmatical realities. “It was gauss who gave them a rigorous construction and their set from
the set of points in the plane and who called them “complex numbers.” He also Anstitutionalized the usage of the
i to denote \~1, an act previously suggested by the Swiss mathematician Argand.

The extension of the set of numbers used by man was largely linked to the solution of equations. The exten-
sion of N into Z can be justified by the need to solve the equation of the form x + a = 0. Similarly, the extension,
of Z into Q may be justified by the need to solve every equation of the form ax + b = 0. The extension of Q into
R is guided by other necessities, particularly the need to assign an abscissa to each point of an axis. The exten-
sion of R into C is necessitated by the need to solve every quadratic equation. We may wonder if the need to solve
a new equation would generate entirely new sets of numbers. The answer to this question is negative however,
since the fundamental theorem of algebra shows that every equation of the form

BOE A Kea tt orld tO,
with complex coefficients has n roots.

INTRODUCTION ———
Group theory has its origins in the study of equations. The
French mathematician Evariste Galois proved that a polyno-
mial equation of the form
ata of he hag hay 0
can be solved using radicals if and only if the set of permuta-
tions of its roots ( that form a group) has a certain property.
Since then, mathematicians have been interested in group
theory whose applications are not confined to mathematics
i alone. Indeed, this theory is used in physics and chemistry
Prerequisites
(crystallography).

Let E be a nonempty set.
@ BINARY OPERATIONS
Definition 1

A binary operation on a set £ is any rule that assigns to

every pair (x,y) © E X E anelementz in £ called the com-

posite of x and y.
Symbols are used to denote a binary operation. If the binary oper- thus addition of real numbers is
ation is denoted by*, then the element z is denoted by x *y . denoted by +.
===END PAGE 135===

===PAGE 136===
To discuss a binary operation means to investigate whether the operation is associative, commutative, has an
identity element, and whether every element has an inverse. The discussion of an operation involves provid-
ing a counterexample that proves, if that is the case, that the operation does not satisfy a certain property.
& Is the rule that assigns x to a pair of integers (x, y) a binary operation on Z* ? Justify your answer.

cuss its properties, otherwise indicate why not.

4 Discuss the properties of the binary operation defined on @ by x # yp = x + a
s Discuss the properties of the binary operation on IB defined by x * y = x + y+ xy.

@ _ DEFINITIONS AND PROPERTIES
In this chapter, the word mapping means a mapping of the plane
onto itself.
Definition 1
is a point and k is a nonzero real number, a dilation with M
center O and ratio (scale factor) k is the mapping that M
assigns to every point M the point M’ such that OM’ = KOM
The dilation with center O and ratio k is denoted by A(O, k). 0
h(O, 1) is the identity mapping of the plane. h(O, —1) is the cen-
tral symmetry with center O, We can easily verify that, when
k #1, O is the only invariant point under A(O, k). h is called a
positive or negative dilation according to whether k is positive or
negative.
A

three vertices onto the midpoints of the opposite sides. 2

Property 4
A dilation with ratio 4 transforms:
- aline onto a line that is either parallel or coinciding with it;
~ a segment of length 4 onto a segment of length |A| 4;
- a directed angle onto a directed angle with the same

- two parallel lines onto two parallel lines;
- two orthogonal lines onto two orthogonal lines.

INTRODUCTION ————
Although it has most of the properties of an isometry, a dila-
tion (homothety) differs from an isometry in its ability to
expand or contract geometric figures. Hence comes its impor-
tance which is particularly noticeable in cartography.
Also note that proofs that make use of a dilation either geo-
metrically, or analytically or through the use of vectors, are
relatively easy.
In this chapter, we will prove the properties of a dilation and
we will discuss the composite of two dilations and of a dila-
tion and a translation.
i. The chapter’s objectives are:
Prerequisites * to characterize a dilation;
* to know and to use the properties of a dilation;
Knowledge:ots * to examine the effect of a dilation on geometric figures;
Elementary operations + to determine the composite of two dilations and of a dilation
on vectors, directed and a translation; :
ee cams * to determine the dilations that interchange two given cir-
cles.

INTRODUCTION —W—
Translations, rotations, reflections and their properties were
studied in the second year secondary. It was proven that all
these transformations are isometries. In this chapter, we shall
complete our study and characterize all the isometries in the
ee lane.
Prerequisites :
This study will allow us to differentiate between two types of
naWeaRe isometries: those that preserve the directed angles and others
that do not.

a «ss
In this chapter, the expression mapping of the plane denotes a
mapping from the plane onto itself. /dp denotes the identity map-

Recall that:
The properties of isometries with respect to preservation can be summarized in the following
table:
Translation | Rotation Reflection Composite of a reflection
and a translation
To determine the elements of an isometry.
+ The translation vector: determine the image 4’ of a special point A, The vector will
be Ad’,
+ The center of rotation: find the invariant point O. The center will be O.
+ The angle of rotation: determine the image (4’, B’) of a special pair (4, B). An angle
will be (AB, 4’B’).
+ The axis of a reflection: determine two distinct invariant points A and B. The axis will
be (AB).

INTRODUCTION ———
Recognizing complex numbers was a controversial issue for
mathematicians until their geometric representation by Gauss
who wrote that: " All quantities both real and imaginary num-
bers can be represented by means of the indefinite.” In the
nineteenth century everyone was charmed by the magical
P rerequi sites character of complex numbers. Their applications in different
mathematical domains remains varied.
Knowledge of We have already used complex numbers in trigonometry and
we intend to show their importance in the study of plane geo-
Algebraic and trigono- metric configurations . |
metric forms of complex
numbers.
i , a : .
ise ata In this chapter, we shall interpret geometrically

INTRODUCTION ——
In meteorology, level curves are the set of points that have the
same temperature. Topographers use this notion to denote the
set of points with the same altitude. In mathematics, level
curves of a mapping fare the curves, or the sets of points /M,
Prerequisites that satisfy {(M) = &, where k is a real constant.

INTRODUCTION ———
To separately use isometries and dilations is not sufficient to
deal with all the possible geometric configurations. A larger
interest lies in the composite of an isometry and a dilation.
Based on the syllabus, our study focuses on the composite of
direct isometries (translations or rotations) with dilations, that
is to say, with direct plane similitudes. We will prove that the
composite of a rotation and a dilation with distinct centers can
be always identified by the composite of a rotation and a dila-
tion with the same center. This identification will be of greater
interest when using and extracting the properties of direct
plane similitudes.
The chapter’s objectives are:
1. to characterize a direct plane similitude, its elements and
Prerequisites the inverse similitude;
2. to know the properties of a direct plane similitude:
Knowledge of: . . . oo
; . 3. to examine the effect of a direct plane similitude on geo-
translations, rotations, metric figures;
dilations, their proper- . . Co.
Hes). ane Te0al Gaizves, 4. to determine the direct plane similitude that transforms a
pair (VM, N) onto another pair (M’, N’).

Uniqueness:

Let S and S’ be two direct similitudes that transform A onto C
and B onto D: S(A) = S’(A) = C and S(B) = S'(B) = D.
Therefore S’ o S-' (C) = C and S’ o S-' (D) = D. Thus S" o S' is
a direct similitude that has two invariant points C and D. This is
only possible if S’ o S-' (C) = Idp, thus S’ = S.
===END PAGE 184===

===PAGE 185===
By its definition, a direct similitude S(O, k, a) multiplies the
distances by & and preserves the measure of directed angles . We
have the following property
Property 4
Every transformation that multiplies distances by  (k > 0)
d pi reserves the measure of directed angles is a direct
imilitude with ratio k.
In particular:
Property 5
ea and a dilation with any centers
Consider a rotation r = r(A, a) and a dilation 4 = A(B, k).
In the special case where A 4B, a = m and k = —1, we can verify that roh
and ho rare two translations with vectors 2B4 and 24B, respectively,
In the other cases, ro and Ao rare direct similitudes with center, of ratio
|| and angle a , or m + a, according to the sign of k.
(@) PROPERTIES OF DIRECT SIMILITUDES
A direct similitude is a translation or the composite of a rotation
and a dilation. It is therefore a bijection.
In addition:
S(O, k, «) « (0, > =a) = W(O, k) or(O, «) 0 (0,~a) oO, +)
= WO, ko HO, _ = Idp.
Hence:

similitudes.

INTRODUCTION ————
The one-to-one correspondence between the points of the
plane and complex numbers leads to the following intuitive
result: when a point moves in the plane, its complex affix
varies in €. Hence the idea to assign to each geometric
transformation in the plane a mapping between the complex
numbers.
Prerequisites This idea proves to be useful when applied to the study of
geometric configurations and to the determination of
Knowledge of: images, using a simple calculation on the complex numbers.
Transformations: transla-
tions, rotations, dilations, The objectives of this chapter are:
and similitudes. 1. to discuss the complex expression of a translation, rota-
Operations on C. tion, dilation, and a direct similitude: :
Different forms of a z | . 4 |
complex number; the 2. to discuss the geometric transformation that has the com-
modulus and argument plex expression: z > az + b;
2, ~ Zu
af Be 3. to discuss the inversion with center O and ratio | as_ well
as its complex form.

(i) A SPECIAL INVERSION -
In this part, P* denotes the plane deprived of the origin O and C*
denotes the set C — {O}.
Definition 2
An inversion with center O and power | is the mapping of
ym P* that, to every point M, assigns the point M’ such that:
j M’«(OM)and OM .OM'=1.
/ This inversion is denoted by /(O, 1) or simply /.

= © CONICS: THE EQUATIONS
INTRODUCTION —
Conics are the curves obtained by cutting a circular cone with
a plane. If the plane passes through the vertex of the cone, the
conic section obtained is said to be degenerate: it is either
reduced to one point that is the vertex of the cone, or is formed
of a pair of distinct or coinciding lines. On the other hand, if
the plane does not pass through the vertex of the cone, the
conic obtained is either: a circle, an ellipse, a parabola, or a
hyperbola, depending on the inclination of the plane. The
Greek mathematician Apollonius (around 260-200 BC) pro-
duced an extensive study of conics.
The importance of conics lies in their fields of application.
The trajectories of the planets, satellites, electrons, and pro-
jectiles are all conics. The parabolic antennas are indispensa-
ble tools for receiving the signals emitted by communication
satellites. Parabolic mirrors are very useful due to their prop- |
Pp isit erties such as that by which a ray parallel to the focal axis is
renequisives reflected to pass through the focus etc.
Ki led; fe
odie In this chapter, we shall:

— 20) CONICS: THE PROPERTIES
INTRODUCTION —
In the preceding chapter we proved the basic equations of the
different types of conics. We also determined the different ele-
ments of each one of these as well as their axes of symmetry.
Finally, we discussed the curves whose equations are
quadratic.
Prerequisites Conics have numerous properties that were applied by man.
The parabolic antennas used to receive television programs
Rea wees emitted by satellites are applications of a very simple proper-
ne ec? ty of parabolas. The section of such an antenna by a plane
Definitions of conics. passing through its axis is a parabola whose focal axis is the
Classification of conics. axis of the antenna. The signals emitted by the satellites arrive
. at the antenna parallel to the focal axis and are reflected to
Basic (reduced) equa- 43 "
. pass through the focus. This property allows the concentration
tions of a parabola, % . z
ellipseand hyperbola of the different signals at a single point: the focus.

SELECTED READING
CONICS

Conics were first studied by the Greeks. Hippocrates of Chios is attributed to have used
the intersections of conics to solve one of the classical problems of Greek geometry.
Appolonius (2nd century BC) produced an extensive study of conic sections. Archimedes (3rd
century BC) determined the area of a surface bounded by a segment of a parabola by ingenius
procedures that anticipated modern infinitesimal calculations.

One of the most important applications of the
theory of conics is related to theoretical mechanics.
Indeed, the trajectories of a point mass, or of the cen- oe
ter of gravity of a solid in a Newtonian field of forces
(a field of forces directed at every point towards a
point O and whose intensity is inversely proportional
to the square of its distance from O) are conics that Sa
have O as a focus.

Thus the orbit of an artificial satellite is an ellipse
that has the center of the mass of the earth as a
focus. Its period is given by the formula

fa

T=20 eh , where 2a is the major axis of the ellipse, m is the mass of the earth, and g is
the universal constant of gravity.

Similarly the orbits of the planets around
the sun are ellipses. On the other hand, the =
majority of comets and nearly two-thirds of ON
the less bright meteorites that seem to come y
from cosmic distances have parabolic or
hyperbolic trajectories. However it is possible @
that many comets with trajectories that are S
assumed to be parabolic in reality move along
extremely elongated elliptical orbits.

The trajectory of Halley’s comet is a very
elongated ellipse. The period of this comet is
76 years. Its appearance near the earth was
recorded in 1532. Its last passage took place
in 1986.
